<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Solar System</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Sun and its planetary system. For other similar systems, see <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">Star system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">Planetary system</a>.</div>

<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_system.jpg"><img alt="Solar system.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="5600" data-file-width="4500" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/13px-Solar_system.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/19px-Solar_system.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/26px-Solar_system.jpg 2x" width="13"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Solar_System" title="Portal:Solar System">Solar System portal</a><br/><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:He1523a.jpg"><img alt="He1523a.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="180" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/He1523a.jpg/14px-He1523a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/He1523a.jpg/21px-He1523a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/He1523a.jpg/28px-He1523a.jpg 2x" width="14"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Star" title="Portal:Star">Star portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Solar_System_sidebar" title="Template:Solar System sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Solar_System_sidebar" title="Template talk:Solar System sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Solar_System_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Solar System</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[a]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitationally</a> bound system of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the objects that orbit it, either directly or indirectly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[b]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/List_of_gravitationally_rounded_objects_of_the_Solar_System#Planets" title="List of gravitationally rounded objects of the Solar System">eight planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_gravitationally_rounded_objects_of_the_Solar_System#Dwarf_planets" title="List of gravitationally rounded objects of the Solar System">five dwarf planets</a> as defined by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU). Of the objects that orbit the Sun directly, the largest eight are the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[c]</a></sup> with the remainder being smaller objects, such as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>. Of the objects that orbit the Sun indirectly, the <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, two are larger than the smallest planet, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[d]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Solar System <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formed 4.6 billion years ago</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of a giant interstellar <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a>. The vast majority of the system's <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is in the Sun, with the majority of the remaining mass contained in <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The four smaller inner planets, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>, being primarily composed of rock and metal. The four outer planets are <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, being substantially more massive than the terrestrials. The two largest, Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>, being composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>; the two outermost planets, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>, being composed mostly of substances with relatively high melting points compared with hydrogen and helium, called <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>, such as water, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. All eight planets have almost circular orbits that lie within a nearly flat disc called the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>.
</p><p>The Solar System also contains smaller objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, which lies between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter, mostly contains objects composed, like the terrestrial planets, of rock and metal. Beyond Neptune's orbit lie the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>, which are populations of <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a> composed mostly of ices, and beyond them a newly discovered population of <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">sednoids</a>. Within these populations are several dozen to possibly tens of thousands of objects large enough that they have been rounded by their own gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2012_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2012-15">[10]</a></sup> Such objects are categorized as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>. Identified dwarf planets include the asteroid <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> and the trans-Neptunian objects <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> In addition to these two regions, various other small-body populations, including <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">interplanetary dust clouds</a>, freely travel between regions. Six of the planets, at least four of the dwarf planets, and many of the smaller bodies are orbited by <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[f]</a></sup> usually termed "moons" after the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Each of the outer planets is encircled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a> of dust and other small objects.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, a stream of charged particles flowing outwards from the Sun, creates a bubble-like region in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a> is the point at which pressure from the solar wind is equal to the opposing pressure of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>; it extends out to the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, which is thought to be the source for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" title="Long-period comet">long-period comets</a>, may also exist at a distance roughly a thousand times further than the heliosphere. The Solar System is located in the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a>, 26,000 light-years from the center of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_exploration">Discovery and exploration</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_and_exploration_of_the_Solar_System" title="Discovery and exploration of the Solar System">Discovery and exploration of the Solar System</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heliocentric.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="570" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/170px-Heliocentric.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/255px-Heliocentric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/340px-Heliocentric.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heliocentric.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Cellarius" title="Andreas Cellarius">Andreas Cellarius</a>'s illustration of the Copernican system, from the <i>Harmonia Macrocosmica</i> (1660)</div></div></div>
<p>For most of history, humanity did not recognize or understand the concept of the Solar System. Most people up to the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>–<a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> believed Earth to be stationary at the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and categorically different from the divine or ethereal objects that moved through the sky. Although the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> had speculated on a heliocentric reordering of the cosmos, <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> was the first to develop a mathematically predictive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 17th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> discovered that the Sun was marked with sunspots, and that Jupiter had four satellites in orbit around it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> followed on from Galileo's discoveries by discovering Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> and the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> realised in 1705 that repeated sightings of <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">a comet</a> were recording the same object, returning regularly once every 75–76 years. This was the first evidence that anything other than the planets orbited the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[15]</a></sup> Around this time (1704), the term "Solar System" first appeared in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[16]</a></sup> In 1838, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel" title="Friedrich Bessel">Friedrich Bessel</a> successfully measured a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">stellar parallax</a>, an apparent shift in the position of a star created by Earth's motion around the Sun, providing the first direct, experimental proof of heliocentrism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[17]</a></sup> Improvements in observational astronomy and the use of <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">unmanned spacecraft</a> have since enabled the detailed investigation of other bodies orbiting the Sun.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_composition">Structure and composition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:908px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar-System.pdf" title="Comprehensive overview of the Solar System. The Sun, planets, dwarf planets and moons are at scale for their relative sizes, not for distances. A separate distance scale is at the bottom. Moons are listed near their planets by proximity of their orbits; only the largest moons are shown."><img alt="" data-file-height="1476" data-file-width="3897" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Solar-System.pdf/page1-900px-Solar-System.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Solar-System.pdf/page1-1350px-Solar-System.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Solar-System.pdf/page1-1800px-Solar-System.pdf.jpg 2x" width="900"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar-System.pdf" title="File:Solar-System.pdf"> </a></div><b>Comprehensive overview of the Solar System.</b> The Sun, planets, dwarf planets and moons are at scale for their relative sizes, not for distances. A separate distance scale is at the bottom. Moons are listed near their planets by proximity of their orbits; only the largest moons are shown.</div>
</div></div>
<p>The principal component of the Solar System is the Sun, a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G2 main-sequence star</a> that contains 99.86% of the system's known mass and dominates it gravitationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[18]</a></sup> The Sun's four largest orbiting bodies, the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, account for 99% of the remaining mass, with Jupiter and Saturn together comprising more than 90%. The remaining objects of the Solar System (including the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons" title="List of moons">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>) together comprise less than 0.002% of the Solar System's total mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteD_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteD-28">[g]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most large objects in orbit around the Sun lie near the plane of Earth's orbit, known as the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. The planets are very close to the ecliptic, whereas comets and <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects are frequently at significantly greater angles to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2003_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2003-29">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[23]</a></sup> All the planets, and most other objects, orbit the Sun in the same direction that the Sun is rotating (counter-clockwise, as viewed from above Earth's north pole).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[24]</a></sup> There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">exceptions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a>.
</p><p>The overall structure of the charted regions of the Solar System consists of the Sun, four relatively small inner planets surrounded by a belt of mostly rocky asteroids, and four giant planets surrounded by the Kuiper belt of mostly icy objects. Astronomers sometimes informally divide this structure into separate regions. The inner Solar System includes the four terrestrial planets and the asteroid belt. The outer Solar System is beyond the asteroids, including the four giant planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[25]</a></sup> Since the discovery of the Kuiper belt, the outermost parts of the Solar System are considered a distinct region consisting of the objects beyond Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the planets in the Solar System have secondary systems of their own, being orbited by planetary objects called <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>, or moons (two of which, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, are larger than the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>), and, in the case of the four giant planets, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a>, thin bands of tiny particles that orbit them in unison. Most of the largest natural satellites are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">synchronous rotation</a>, with one face permanently turned toward their parent.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:285px;max-width:285px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="249" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif/140px-Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif/210px-Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Ecliptic_plane_3d_view.gif 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:139px;max-width:139px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="248" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif/137px-Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif/206px-Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 2x" width="137"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">All planets of the Solar System lie very close to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. The closer they are to the Sun, the faster they travel <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_planets" title="Inner planets">inner planets</a> on the left, all planets except Neptune on the right)</i>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a> describe the orbits of objects about the Sun. Following Kepler's laws, each object travels along an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> with the Sun at one <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">focus</a>. Objects closer to the Sun (with smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a>) travel more quickly because they are more affected by the Sun's gravity. On an elliptical orbit, a body's distance from the Sun varies over the course of its year. A body's closest approach to the Sun is called its <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a></i>, whereas its most distant point from the Sun is called its <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a></i>. The orbits of the planets are nearly circular, but many comets, asteroids, and Kuiper belt objects follow highly elliptical orbits. The positions of the bodies in the Solar System can be predicted using <a href="/wiki/Numerical_model_of_the_Solar_System" title="Numerical model of the Solar System">numerical models</a>.
</p><p>Although the Sun dominates the system by mass, it accounts for only about 2% of the <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marochnik1995_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marochnik1995-34">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[28]</a></sup> The planets, dominated by Jupiter, account for most of the rest of the angular momentum due to the combination of their mass, orbit, and distance from the Sun, with a possibly significant contribution from comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marochnik1995_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marochnik1995-34">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sun, which comprises nearly all the matter in the Solar System, is composed of roughly 98% hydrogen and helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, which comprise nearly all the remaining matter, are also primarily composed of hydrogen and helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[31]</a></sup> A composition gradient exists in the Solar System, created by heat and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_pressure" title="Light pressure">light pressure</a> from the Sun; those objects closer to the Sun, which are more affected by heat and light pressure, are composed of elements with high melting points. Objects farther from the Sun are composed largely of materials with lower melting points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[32]</a></sup> The boundary in the Solar System beyond which those volatile substances could condense is known as the <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">frost line</a>, and it lies at roughly 5 AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumma_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mumma-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The objects of the inner Solar System are composed mostly of rock,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-40">[33]</a></sup> the collective name for compounds with high melting points, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicates" title="Silicates">silicates</a>, iron or nickel, that remained solid under almost all conditions in the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_nebula" title="Protoplanetary nebula">protoplanetary nebula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-41">[34]</a></sup> Jupiter and Saturn are composed mainly of gases, the astronomical term for materials with extremely low melting points and high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapour_pressure" title="Vapour pressure">vapour pressure</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, which were always in the gaseous phase in the nebula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-41">[34]</a></sup> Ices, like <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-40">[33]</a></sup> have melting points up to a few hundred kelvins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-41">[34]</a></sup> They can be found as ices, liquids, or gases in various places in the Solar System, whereas in the nebula they were either in the solid or gaseous phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-41">[34]</a></sup> Icy substances comprise the majority of the satellites of the giant planets, as well as most of Uranus and Neptune (the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>") and the numerous small objects that lie beyond Neptune's orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-40">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeilik_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik-42">[35]</a></sup> Together, gases and ices are referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Placxo_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Placxo-43">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distances_and_scales">Distances and scales</span></h3>
<p>The distance from Earth to the Sun is 1 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a> [AU] (150,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a>; 93,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">mi</a>). For comparison, the radius of the Sun is 0.0047 AU (700,000 km). Thus, the Sun occupies 0.00001% (10<sup>−5</sup> %) of the volume of a sphere with a radius the size of Earth's orbit, whereas Earth's volume is roughly one millionth (10<sup>−6</sup>) that of the Sun. Jupiter, the largest planet, is 5.2 astronomical units (780,000,000 km) from the Sun and has a radius of 71,000 km (0.00047 AU), whereas the most distant planet, Neptune, is 30 AU (4.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> km) from the Sun.
</p><p>With a few exceptions, the farther a planet or belt is from the Sun, the larger the distance between its orbit and the orbit of the next nearer object to the Sun. For example, Venus is approximately 0.33 AU farther out from the Sun than Mercury, whereas Saturn is 4.3 AU out from Jupiter, and Neptune lies 10.5 AU out from Uranus. Attempts have been made to determine a relationship between these orbital distances (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Titius%E2%80%93Bode_law" title="Titius–Bode law">Titius–Bode law</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[37]</a></sup> but no such theory has been accepted. The images at the beginning of this section show the orbits of the various constituents of the Solar System on different scales.
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Solar_System_model" title="Solar System model">Solar System models</a> attempt to convey the relative scales involved in the Solar System on human terms. Some are small in scale (and may be mechanical—called <a href="/wiki/Orrery" title="Orrery">orreries</a>)—whereas others extend across cities or regional areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[38]</a></sup> The largest such scale model, the <a href="/wiki/Sweden_Solar_System" title="Sweden Solar System">Sweden Solar System</a>, uses the 110-metre (361 ft) <a href="/wiki/Ericsson_Globe" title="Ericsson Globe">Ericsson Globe</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> as its substitute Sun, and, following the scale, Jupiter is a 7.5-metre (25-foot) sphere at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlanda_International_Airport" title="Arlanda International Airport">Arlanda International Airport</a>, 40 km (25 mi) away, whereas the farthest current object, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, is a 10 cm (4 in) sphere in <a href="/wiki/Lule%C3%A5" title="Luleå">Luleå</a>, 912 km (567 mi) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sedna_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sedna-47">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the Sun–Neptune distance is <a href="/wiki/Solar_System_model" title="Solar System model">scaled</a> to 100 metres, then the Sun would be about 3 cm in diameter (roughly two-thirds the diameter of a golf ball), the giant planets would be all smaller than about 3 mm, and Earth's diameter along with that of the other terrestrial planets would be smaller than a flea (0.3 mm) at this scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:5008px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_System_Objects_in_Scale.jpg" title="The Solar System. Distances are to scale, objects are not.</div></div>
</div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
