font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #FF950A; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#FFB657; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">Scattered disc</a></span><br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #550055; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#A300A3; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">Neptune trojans</a></span> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1995" data-file-width="2750" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/220px-EightTNOs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/330px-EightTNOs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/440px-EightTNOs.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size comparison of some large <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">TNOs</a> with Earth: <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">its moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_OR10" title="2007 OR10">2007 OR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">10</span></span></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Kuiper belt is a great ring of debris similar to the asteroid belt, but consisting mainly of objects composed primarily of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physical_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-physical-116">[108]</a></sup> It extends between 30 and 50 AU from the Sun. Though it is estimated to contain anything from dozens to thousands of dwarf planets, it is composed mainly of small Solar System bodies. Many of the larger Kuiper belt objects, such as <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/20000_Varuna" title="20000 Varuna">Varuna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, may prove to be dwarf planets with further data. There are estimated to be over 100,000 Kuiper belt objects with a diameter greater than 50 km, but the total mass of the Kuiper belt is thought to be only a tenth or even a hundredth the mass of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets-26">[20]</a></sup> Many Kuiper belt objects have multiple satellites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup> and most have orbits that take them outside the plane of the ecliptic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trojan_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-trojan-118">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Kuiper belt can be roughly divided into the "<a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">classical</a>" belt and the <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">resonances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physical_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-physical-116">[108]</a></sup> Resonances are orbits linked to that of Neptune (e.g. twice for every three Neptune orbits, or once for every two). The first resonance begins within the orbit of Neptune itself. The classical belt consists of objects having no resonance with Neptune, and extends from roughly 39.4 AU to 47.7 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup> Members of the classical Kuiper belt are classified as <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">cubewanos</a>, after the first of their kind to be discovered, <a href="/wiki/15760_Albion" title="15760 Albion">15760 Albion</a> (which previously had the provisional designation 1992 QB<sub>1</sub>), and are still in near primordial, low-eccentricity orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_and_Charon">Pluto and Charon</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon (moon)</a></div>
<dl><dd>The dwarf planet Pluto (39 AU average) is the largest known object in the Kuiper belt. When discovered in 1930, it was considered to be the ninth planet; this changed in 2006 with the adoption of a formal <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">definition of planet</a>. Pluto has a relatively eccentric orbit inclined 17 degrees to the ecliptic plane and ranging from 29.7 AU from the Sun at perihelion (within the orbit of Neptune) to 49.5 AU at aphelion. Pluto has a 3:2 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Neptune, meaning that Pluto orbits twice round the Sun for every three Neptunian orbits. Kuiper belt objects whose orbits share this resonance are called <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fajans_et_al_2001_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fajans_et_al_2001-121">[113]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>Charon, the largest of <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">Pluto's moons</a>, is sometimes described as part of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy)" title="Binary system (astronomy)">binary system</a> with Pluto, as the two bodies orbit a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentre" title="Barycentre">barycentre</a> of gravity above their surfaces (i.e. they appear to "orbit each other"). Beyond Charon, four much smaller moons, <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>, orbit within the system.</dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Makemake_and_Haumea">Makemake and Haumea</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a></div>
<dl><dd>Makemake (45.79 AU average), although smaller than Pluto, is the largest known object in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object"><i>classical</i> Kuiper belt</a> (that is, a Kuiper belt object not in a confirmed <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">resonance</a> with Neptune). Makemake is the brightest object in the Kuiper belt after Pluto. It was named and designated a dwarf planet in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-name-11">[7]</a></sup> Its orbit is far more inclined than Pluto's, at 29°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie136472_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie136472-122">[114]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>Haumea (43.13 AU average) is in an orbit similar to Makemake except that it is in a 7:12 orbital resonance with Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brownlargest_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-brownlargest-123">[115]</a></sup> It is about the same size as Makemake and has two natural satellites. A rapid, 3.9-hour rotation gives it a flattened and elongated shape. It was named and designated a dwarf planet in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaunews_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaunews-124">[116]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scattered_disc">Scattered disc</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">Scattered disc</a></div>
<p>The scattered disc, which overlaps the Kuiper belt but extends much further outwards, is thought to be the source of short-period comets. Scattered-disc objects are thought to have been ejected into erratic orbits by the gravitational influence of <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System#Planetary_migration" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Neptune's early outward migration</a>. Most scattered disc objects (SDOs) have perihelia within the Kuiper belt but aphelia far beyond it (some more than 150 AU from the Sun). SDOs' orbits are also highly inclined to the ecliptic plane and are often almost perpendicular to it. Some astronomers consider the scattered disc to be merely another region of the Kuiper belt and describe scattered disc objects as "scattered Kuiper belt objects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> Some astronomers also classify centaurs as inward-scattered Kuiper belt objects along with the outward-scattered residents of the scattered disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eris">Eris</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris (dwarf planet)</a></div>
<dl><dd>Eris (68 AU average) is the largest known scattered disc object, and caused a debate about what constitutes a planet, because it is 25% more massive than Pluto<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Schaller_2007_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Schaller_2007-127">[119]</a></sup> and about the same diameter. It is the most massive of the known dwarf planets. It has one known moon, <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a>. Like Pluto, its orbit is highly eccentric, with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> of 38.2 AU (roughly Pluto's distance from the Sun) and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> of 97.6 AU, and steeply inclined to the ecliptic plane.</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Farthest_regions">Farthest regions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solarmap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="605" data-file-width="1000" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Solarmap.png/220px-Solarmap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Solarmap.png/330px-Solarmap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Solarmap.png/440px-Solarmap.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solarmap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From the Sun to the nearest star: The Solar System on a <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic scale</a> in <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU)</div></div></div>
<p>The point at which the Solar System ends and interstellar space begins is not precisely defined because its outer boundaries are shaped by two separate forces: the solar wind and the Sun's gravity. The limit of the solar wind's influence is roughly four times Pluto's distance from the Sun; this <i>heliopause</i>, the outer boundary of the <a href="#Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>, is considered the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-70">[62]</a></sup> The Sun's <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a>, the effective range of its gravitational dominance, is thought to extend up to a thousand times farther and encompasses the theorized <a href="#Oort_cloud">Oort cloud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Littmann_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Littmann-128">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heliosphere"><span id="Heliopause"></span> Heliosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">Heliosphere</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NewHeliopause_558121.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/NewHeliopause_558121.jpg/220px-NewHeliopause_558121.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/NewHeliopause_558121.jpg/330px-NewHeliopause_558121.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/NewHeliopause_558121.jpg/440px-NewHeliopause_558121.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NewHeliopause_558121.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bubble-like <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a> with its various transitional regions moving through the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a></div></div></div>
<p>The heliosphere is a <a href="/wiki/Stellar-wind_bubble" title="Stellar-wind bubble">stellar-wind bubble</a>, a region of space dominated by the Sun, which radiates at roughly 400 km/s its <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, a stream of charged particles, until it collides with the wind of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.
</p><p>The collision occurs at the <i>termination shock</i>, which is roughly 80–100 AU from the Sun upwind of the interstellar medium and roughly 200 AU from the Sun downwind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fahr_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-fahr-129">[121]</a></sup> Here the wind slows dramatically, condenses and becomes more turbulent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fahr_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-fahr-129">[121]</a></sup> forming a great oval structure known as the <i>heliosheath</i>. This structure is thought to look and behave very much like a comet's tail, extending outward for a further 40 AU on the upwind side but tailing many times that distance downwind; evidence from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_(spacecraft)" title="Cassini (spacecraft)">Cassini</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_Boundary_Explorer" title="Interstellar Boundary Explorer">Interstellar Boundary Explorer</a> spacecraft has suggested that it is forced into a bubble shape by the constraining action of the interstellar magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The outer boundary of the heliosphere, the <i>heliopause</i>, is the point at which the solar wind finally terminates and is the beginning of interstellar space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-70">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a> are reported to have passed the termination shock and entered the heliosheath, at 94 and 84 AU from the Sun, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[124]</a></sup> Voyager 1 is reported to have crossed the heliopause in August 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130912_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130912-133">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The shape and form of the outer edge of the heliosphere is likely affected by the <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a> of interactions with the interstellar medium as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_magnetic_field" title="Solar magnetic field">solar magnetic fields</a> prevailing to the south, e.g. it is bluntly shaped with the northern hemisphere extending 9 AU farther than the southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fahr_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-fahr-129">[121]</a></sup> Beyond the heliopause, at around 230 AU, lies the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a>, a plasma "wake" left by the Sun as it travels through the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2499" data-file-width="2499" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/220px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/330px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/440px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zooming out the Solar System: <div><ul><li>inner Solar System and Jupiter</li><li>outer Solar System and Pluto</li><li>orbit of Sedna (detached object)</li><li>inner part of the Oort Cloud</li></ul></div></div></div></div>
<p>Due to a lack of data, conditions in local interstellar space are not known for certain. It is expected that <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager spacecraft</a>, as they pass the heliopause, will transmit valuable data on radiation levels and solar wind to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup> How well the heliosphere shields the Solar System from cosmic rays is poorly understood. A NASA-funded team has developed a concept of a "Vision Mission" dedicated to sending a probe to the heliosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detached_objects">Detached objects</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Detached_object" title="Detached object">Detached object</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">Sednoid</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">90377 Sedna</a> (520 AU average) is a large, reddish object with a gigantic, highly elliptical orbit that takes it from about 76 AU at perihelion to 940 AU at aphelion and takes 11,400 years to complete. <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a>, who discovered the object in 2003, asserts that it cannot be part of the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> or the Kuiper belt because its perihelion is too distant to have been affected by Neptune's migration. He and other astronomers consider it to be the first in an entirely new population, sometimes termed "distant detached objects" (DDOs), which also may include the object <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(148209)_2000_CR105" title="(148209) 2000 CR105">2000 CR<sub>105</sub></a></span>, which has a perihelion of 45 AU, an aphelion of 415 AU, and an orbital period of 3,420 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[130]</a></sup> Brown terms this population the "inner Oort cloud" because it may have formed through a similar process, although it is far closer to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[131]</a></sup> Sedna is very likely a dwarf planet, though its shape has yet to be determined. The second unequivocally detached object, with a perihelion farther than Sedna's at roughly 81 AU, is <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2012_VP113" title="2012 VP113">2012 VP<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">113</span></span></a></span>, discovered in 2012. Its aphelion is only half that of Sedna's, at 400–500 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_2012_VP113_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata_2012_VP113-140">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physorg_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physorg-141">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oort_cloud">Oort cloud</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="512" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/220px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/330px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/440px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of the hypothetical <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a></span>, with a spherical outer cloud and a disc-shaped inner cloud</div></div></div>
<p>The Oort cloud is a hypothetical spherical cloud of up to a trillion icy objects that is thought to be the source for all long-period comets and to surround the Solar System at roughly 50,000 AU (around 1 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-year</a> (ly)), and possibly to as far as 100,000 AU (1.87 ly). It is thought to be composed of comets that were ejected from the inner Solar System by gravitational interactions with the outer planets. Oort cloud objects move very slowly, and can be perturbed by infrequent events, such as collisions, the gravitational effects of a passing star, or the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">galactic tide</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal force</a> exerted by the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundaries">Boundaries</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vulcanoid" title="Vulcanoid">Vulcanoid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">Planets beyond Neptune</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Planet_Nine" title="Planet Nine">Planet Nine</a></div>
<p>Much of the Solar System is still unknown. The Sun's gravitational field is estimated to dominate the gravitational forces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">surrounding stars</a> out to about two light years (125,000 AU). Lower estimates for the radius of the Oort cloud, by contrast, do not place it farther than 50,000 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup> Despite discoveries such as Sedna, the region between the Kuiper belt and the Oort cloud, an area tens of thousands of AU in radius, is still virtually unmapped. There are also ongoing studies of the region between Mercury and the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[137]</a></sup> Objects may yet be discovered in the Solar System's uncharted regions.
</p><p>Currently, the furthest known objects, such as <a href="/wiki/Comet_West" title="Comet West">Comet West</a>, have aphelia around 70,000 AU from the Sun, but as the Oort cloud becomes better known, this may change.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_context">Galactic context</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner noresize" style="width:222px;"><div class="thumbimage" style="width:220px;"><div style="position: relative; width: 220px;"><div style="position: absolute; left: 102px; top: 32px; padding: 0;"><img alt="" data-file-height="1045" data-file-width="518" height="32" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Yellow_Arrow_Down.png/16px-Yellow_Arrow_Down.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Yellow_Arrow_Down.png/24px-Yellow_Arrow_Down.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Yellow_Arrow_Down.png/32px-Yellow_Arrow_Down.png 2x" width="16"/></div><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg" title="Position of the Solar System within the Milky Way"><img alt="Position of the Solar System within the Milky Way" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="512" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/220px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/330px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg/440px-Milky_Way_Arms_ssc2008-10.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Diagram of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> with the position of the Solar System marked by a yellow arrow</div></div></div>
<p>The Solar System is located in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred spiral galaxy</a> with a diameter of about 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> containing about 100 billion stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn9_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn9-146">[138]</a></sup> The Sun resides in one of the Milky Way's outer spiral arms, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion%E2%80%93Cygnus_Arm" title="Orion–Cygnus Arm">Orion–Cygnus Arm</a> or Local Spur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup> The Sun lies between 25,000 and 28,000 light-years from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_Centre" title="Galactic Centre">Galactic Centre</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distance2_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-distance2-148">[140]</a></sup> and its speed within the Milky Way is about 220 km/s, so that it completes one revolution every 225–250 million years. This revolution is known as the Solar System's <a href="/wiki/Galactic_year" title="Galactic year">galactic year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Solar_apex" title="Solar apex">solar apex</a>, the direction of the Sun's path through interstellar space, is near the constellation <a href="/wiki/Hercules_(constellation)" title="Hercules (constellation)">Hercules</a> in the direction of the current location of the bright star <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup> The plane of the ecliptic lies at an angle of about 60° to the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[i]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Solar System's location in the Milky Way is a factor in the <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">evolutionary history of life</a> on Earth. Its orbit is close to circular, and orbits near the Sun are at roughly the same speed as that of the spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-153">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[145]</a></sup> Therefore, the Sun passes through arms only rarely. Because spiral arms are home to a far larger concentration of <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>, gravitational instabilities, and radiation that could disrupt the Solar System, this has given Earth long periods of stability for life to evolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-153">[144]</a></sup> The Solar System also lies well outside the star-crowded environs of the galactic centre. Near the centre, gravitational tugs from nearby stars could perturb bodies in the <a href="#Oort_cloud">Oort cloud</a> and send many comets into the inner Solar System, producing collisions with potentially catastrophic implications for life on Earth. The intense radiation of the galactic centre could also interfere with the development of complex life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-153">[144]</a></sup> Even at the Solar System's current location, some scientists have speculated that recent supernovae may have adversely affected life in the last 35,000 years, by flinging pieces of expelled stellar core towards the Sun, as radioactive dust grains and larger, comet-like bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neighbourhood">Neighbourhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="4000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg/220px-Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg/330px-Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg/440px-Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Local_Interstellar_Clouds_with_motion_arrows.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beyond the heliosphere is the interstellar medium, consisting of various clouds of gases. The Solar System currently moves through the <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">Local Interstellar Cloud</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Solar System is in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">Local Interstellar Cloud</a> or Local Fluff. It is thought to be near the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/G-Cloud" title="G-Cloud">G-Cloud</a> but it is not known if the Solar System is embedded in the Local Interstellar Cloud, or if it is in the region where the Local Interstellar Cloud and G-Cloud are interacting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[148]</a></sup> The Local Interstellar Cloud is an area of denser cloud in an otherwise sparse region known as the <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">Local Bubble</a>, an hourglass-shaped cavity in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> roughly 300 light-years (ly) across. The bubble is suffused with high-temperature plasma, that suggests it is the product of several recent supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are relatively few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">stars within ten light-years of the Sun</a>. The closest is the triple star system <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri</a>, which is about 4.4 light-years away. Alpha Centauri A and B are a closely tied pair of Sun-like stars, whereas the small <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>, orbits the pair at a distance of 0.2 light-year. In 2016, a potentially habitable <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> was confirmed to be orbiting Proxima Centauri, called <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a>, the closest confirmed exoplanet to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[150]</a></sup> The stars next closest to the Sun are the red dwarfs <a href="/wiki/Barnard%27s_Star" title="Barnard's Star">Barnard's Star</a> (at 5.9 ly), <a href="/wiki/Wolf_359" title="Wolf 359">Wolf 359</a> (7.8 ly), and <a href="/wiki/Lalande_21185" title="Lalande 21185">Lalande 21185</a> (8.3 ly).
</p><p>The largest nearby star is <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a>, a bright <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star roughly 8.6 light-years away and roughly twice the Sun's mass and that is orbited by a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>, Sirius B. The nearest <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a> are the binary <a href="/wiki/Luhman_16" title="Luhman 16">Luhman 16</a> system at 6.6 light-years. Other systems within ten light-years are the binary red-dwarf system <a href="/wiki/Luyten_726-8" title="Luyten 726-8">Luyten 726-8</a> (8.7 ly) and the solitary red dwarf <a href="/wiki/Ross_154" title="Ross 154">Ross 154</a> (9.7 ly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[151]</a></sup> The closest solitary Sun-like star to the Solar System is <a href="/wiki/Tau_Ceti" title="Tau Ceti">Tau Ceti</a> at 11.9 light-years. It has roughly 80% of the Sun's mass but only 60% of its luminosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[152]</a></sup> The closest known <a href="/wiki/Rogue_planet" title="Rogue planet">free-floating planetary-mass object</a> to the Sun is <a href="/wiki/WISE_0855%E2%88%920714" title="WISE 0855−0714">WISE 0855−0714</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[153]</a></sup> an object with a mass less than 10 Jupiter masses roughly 7 light-years away.
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:2008px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg" title="A diagram of Earth's location in the observable Universe. (Click here for an alternate image.)"><img alt="" data-file-height="2953" data-file-width="23624" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg/2000px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg/3000px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg/4000px-Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe.jpg 2x" width="2000"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_(JPEG).jpg" title="File:Earth's Location in the Universe (JPEG).jpg"> </a></div>A diagram of Earth's location in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observable_Universe" title="Observable Universe">observable Universe</a>. (<i><a href="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Location_in_the_Universe_SMALLER_(JPEG).jpg" title="File:Earth's Location in the Universe SMALLER (JPEG).jpg">Click here for an alternate image</a>.</i>)</div>
</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_extrasolar_systems">Comparison with extrasolar systems</span></h3>
<p>Compared to other <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary systems</a> the Solar System stands out in lacking planets interior to the orbit of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin082015_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin082015-163">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[155]</a></sup> The known Solar System also lacks <a href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">super-Earths</a> (<a href="/wiki/Planet_Nine" title="Planet Nine">Planet Nine</a> could be a super-Earth beyond the known Solar System).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin082015_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin082015-163">[154]</a></sup> Uncommonly, it has only small rocky planets and large gas giants; elsewhere planets of intermediate size are typical—both rocky and gas—so there is no "gap" as seen between the size of Earth and of Neptune (with a radius 3.8 times as large). Also, these super-Earths have closer orbits than Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin082015_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-Martin082015-163">[154]</a></sup> This led to hypothesis that all planetary systems start with many close-in planets, and that typically a sequence of their collisions causes consolidation of mass into few larger planets, but in case of the Solar System the collisions caused their destruction and ejection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>The orbits of Solar System planets are nearly circular. Compared to other systems, they have smaller <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin082015_163-3"><a href="#cite_note-Martin082015-163">[154]</a></sup> Although there are attempts to explain it partly with a bias in the radial-velocity detection method and partly with long interactions of a quite high number of planets, the exact causes remain undetermined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin082015_163-4"><a href="#cite_note-Martin082015-163">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_summary">Visual summary</span></h2>
<p>This section is a sampling of Solar System bodies, selected for size and quality of imagery, and sorted by volume. Some omitted objects are larger than the ones included here, notably <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, because these have not been imaged in high quality.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Capitalization" title="Capitalization">Capitalization</a> of the name varies. The <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>, the authoritative body regarding astronomical nomenclature, specifies capitalizing the names of all individual astronomical objects, but uses mixed "Solar System" and "solar system" in their <a class="external text" href="http://www.iau.org/public/themes/naming/" rel="nofollow">naming guidelines document</a>. The name is commonly rendered in lower case ("<b>solar system</b>"), as, for example, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.m-w.com/dictionary/solar%20system" rel="nofollow"><i>Merriam-Webster's 11th Collegiate Dictionary</i></a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_satellites" title="Natural satellites">natural satellites</a> (moons) orbiting the Solar System's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun%27s_planets" title="Sun's planets">planets</a> are an example of the latter.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historically, several other bodies were once considered planets, including, from its discovery in 1930 until 2006, <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Former_planets" title="Former planets">Former planets</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The two moons larger than Mercury are <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, which orbits <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, which orbits <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>. Although bigger than Mercury, both moons have less than half the mass of Mercury.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteB-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_14-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_14-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_14-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">IAU definitions</a>, objects orbiting the Sun are classified dynamically and physically into three categories: <i>planets</i>, <i>dwarf planets</i>, and <i>small Solar System bodies</i>.
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> is any body orbiting the Sun whose <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is sufficient for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravity</a> to have pulled it into a (near-)<a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spherical</a> shape and that has <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">cleared its immediate neighbourhood</a> of all smaller objects. By this definition, the Solar System has eight planets: Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune. Because it has not cleared its neighbourhood of other <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects, Pluto does not fit this definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-10">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> is a body orbiting the Sun that is massive enough to be made near-spherical by its own gravity but that has not cleared <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a> from its neighbourhood and is also not a satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-10">[6]</a></sup> Pluto is a dwarf planet and the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">IAU</a> has recognized four other dwarf planets in the Solar System: <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-name-11">[7]</a></sup> Other objects commonly (but not officially) treated as dwarf planets include <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_OR10" title="2007 OR10">2007 OR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">10</span></span></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[8]</a></sup> In a reference to Pluto, other dwarf planets orbiting in the <a href="#Trans-Neptunian_region">trans-Neptunian region</a> are sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoids</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0804_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0804-13">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>The remaining objects orbiting the Sun are known as <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-10">[6]</a></sup> </li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites_of_the_Solar_System" title="List of natural satellites of the Solar System">List of natural satellites of the Solar System</a> for the full list of natural satellites of the eight planets and first five dwarf planets</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteD-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteD_28-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteD_28-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The mass of the Solar System excluding the Sun, Jupiter and Saturn can be determined by adding together all the calculated masses for its largest objects and using rough calculations for the masses of the Oort cloud (estimated at roughly 3 Earth masses),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[19]</a></sup> the Kuiper belt (estimated at roughly 0.1 Earth mass)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets-26">[20]</a></sup> and the asteroid belt (estimated to be 0.0005 Earth mass)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krasinsky2002_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krasinsky2002-27">[21]</a></sup> for a total, rounded upwards, of ~37 Earth masses, or 8.1% of the mass in orbit around the Sun. With the combined masses of Uranus and Neptune (~31 Earth masses) subtracted, the remaining ~6 Earth masses of material comprise 1.3% of the total orbiting mass.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The date is based on the oldest <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_(mineral)" title="Inclusion (mineral)">inclusions</a> found to date in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003456820000000000♠</span>4<span style="margin-left:.25em;">568</span>.2<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+0.2<br/>−0.4</span></span></span> million years, and is thought to be the date of the formation of the first solid material in the collapsing nebula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[42]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">If <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \psi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

padding:0;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="_Celestial_objects_within_0–5_light-years_→" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em">    <a href="/wiki/Template:Star_systems_within_0%E2%80%935_light-years" title="Template:Star systems within 0–5 light-years">Celestial objects within 0–5 light-years</a> <a href="/wiki/Template:Star_systems_within_5%E2%80%9310_light-years" title="Template:Star systems within 5–10 light-years">→</a></div></th></tr><tr><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em"></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-group" id="Primary_member_type" scope="row" style="width:17em"><small><span style="font-weight:normal;"><abbr title="Primary member of a system is the brightest one (and, in most cases, also the most massive one).">Primary</abbr> member <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">type</a></span></small></th><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em"></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2" style="text-align:left;"><div id="Celestial_objects_by_systems._Secondary_members_are_listed_in_small_print."><small>Celestial objects by systems. Secondary members are listed in small print.</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div></div></td></tr><tr><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em"></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-group" id="Main-sequencestars" scope="row" style="width:8em;line-height:0.7em;"><span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">Main-sequence<br/>stars</a></span></th><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em"></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:6.85em;line-height:0.7em;"><span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G-type</a></span></th><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun (<b>0 ly</b>)</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><abbr title="8 planets; over 700,000 minor planets (including 5 dwarf planets); over 5000 comets; 173 moons of planets; over 200 moons of minor planets (including 9 moons of dwarf planets); bodies of planetary rings (mostly were observed rings, not separate bodies, but over 150 moonlets were observed); numerous small bodies, observed as meteors and meteorites, but not catalogued as minor planets or comets; spacecraft; space debris; cosmonauts. Some known comets and spacecrafts, currently located within Solar System boundaries, are not gravitationally bound with the Sun, and therefore formally are separate (interstellar) objects.">rest</abbr> of <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Solar System</a></span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">α Cen (<b>4.366</b><span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em">±</span>0.007<b> ly</b>)</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:85%;">K-type main-sequence star</span><span style="font-size:85%;"> B</span></li>
<li><span style="font-size:85%;">red dwarf</span><span style="font-size:85%;"> <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">C (Proxima Centauri) (<b>4.2430</b> ± 0.0017 <b>ly</b>)</a></span></li>
<li><span style="font-size:85%;">planet</span><span style="font-size:85%;"> <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">b</a></span></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table><div></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div></div></td></tr><tr><td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2"><div><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Italic</i> are systems without known <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">trigonometric parallax</a>.</span></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
